class="hidden" property="articleSection">research-article</div><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D7114%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DGanesh%2BKumar%2BVeerasubramani%252C%2BMyung-Soo%2BPark%252C%2BJin-Yi%2BChoi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D6%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b18997%26title%3DUltrasmall%2BSnS%2BQuantum%2BDots%2BAnchored%2Bonto%2BNitrogen-Enriched%2BCarbon%2BNanospheres%2Bas%2Ban%2BAdvanced%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D7124%26publicationDate%3DFebruary%2B2020" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/medium/am9b18997_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b18997&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Structural pulverization of metal chalcogenides such as Sn-based compounds is a serious issue for development of high-performance anode materials and results in serious capacity fading during continuous charge and discharge cycles. In this work, we synthesize ultrasmall SnS quantum dots (QDs) anchored onto nitrogen-enriched carbon (NC) nanospheres through facile hydrothermal and carbonization processes to prepare a progressive anode material for sodium-ion batteries. The optimized SnS QDs@NC electrode delivered an initial discharge capacity of 281 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and exhibited excellent cycling stability with a capacity retention of 75% after 500 cycles at a high current density of 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Ex situ XRD, XPS, FE-SEM, TEM measurements, and kinetics study were performed to unveil the sodium storage mechanism of the SnS QDs@NC electrode. A sodium-ion full cell assembled with an SnS QDs@NC anode and a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode exhibited high capacity and good cycling stability. Such a superior electrochemical performance of SnS QDs@NC can be attributed to the synergistic effects of NC and SnS QDs where NC serves as a conducting matrix to support SnS QDs and helps avoid structural degradation. This work provides a promising strategy to resolve the pulverization issue of alloying and conversion-type anode materials.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D7114%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DGanesh%2BKumar%2BVeerasubramani%252C%2BMyung-Soo%2BPark%252C%2BJin-Yi%2BChoi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D6%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b18997%26title%3DUltrasmall%2BSnS%2BQuantum%2BDots%2BAnchored%2Bonto%2BNitrogen-Enriched%2BCarbon%2BNanospheres%2Bas%2Ban%2BAdvanced%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D7124%26publicationDate%3DFebruary%2B2020" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292609&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanospheres" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanospheres" class="article__tags__link">Nanospheres</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Physical and chemical processes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Physical%20and%20chemical%20processes" class="article__tags__link">Physical and chemical processes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291975&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Quantum dots" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Quantum%20dots" class="article__tags__link">Quantum dots</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sodium" class="article__tags__link">Sodium</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sns+Quantum+Dot&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_SnS%20quantum%20dot">SnS quantum dot</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon+Nanosphere&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbon%20nanosphere">carbon nanosphere</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode+Material&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode%20material">anode material</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Energy+Storage&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_energy%20storage">energy storage</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Over the past two decades, there has been an increasing demand for energy conversion and storage systems to address environmental pollution and climate change; thus, it is essential to reduce consumption of fossil fuels and move toward more renewable and ecofriendly energy sources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Energy storage devices have played an essential role in storing energy produced by renewable energy sources. So far, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have dominated as power sources for electric vehicles, portable electronics, and grid-scale energy storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 to 5">(3−5)</a> However, in view of the elemental abundance of sodium in the Earth’s crust, which can lead to cost reduction, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are a strong potential substitute for Li-ion technology. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6,7)</a> This has led to increased research interest in SIBs; however, finding advanced electrode materials that can serve as better hosts for sodium ions is still challenging. Additionally, exploration of high-capacity electrode materials that can increase the energy density of SIBs is also urgent.</div><div class="NLM_p">Many efforts have been exerted to exploring high-performance anode materials for SIBs, such as alloy materials, carbonaceous materials, transition-metal sulfides (TMSs), and selenides. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 8 to 15">(8−15)</a> Among these, TMSs are kinetically more beneficial to sodiation and desodiation reactions because of their weaker M–S bonds, facilitating better reversibility and high theoretical capacity. For example, MoS<sub>2</sub>, Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, FeS, FeS<sub>2</sub>, SnS<sub>2</sub>, and SnS have been actively investigated as SIB anode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 16 to 22">(16−22)</a> Among them, tin sulfides (SnS<sub>2</sub> and SnS) are prominent and potential alternatives for hosting larger sodium ions (Na<sup>+</sup>) owing to their layered structures and relatively large interlayer spacing (4.33 Å for SnS and 5.90 Å for SnS<sub>2</sub> vs 1.02 Å for Na<sup>+</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> It is well known that the former material undergoes a smaller volume change (SnS; 242%) than the latter (SnS<sub>2</sub>; 324%) with a lower phase transformation during continuous charge and discharge cycles; thus, it makes more sense to use SnS as a high-stability electrode material even though it has a lower specific capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Regardless of its benefits, SnS suffers from large volume changes, like other TMSs, and shows sluggish kinetics, which result in huge polarizations and limited utilization of active materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26,27)</a> To solve these issues, reducing the size of the SnS material to the quantum dot (QD) scale can be an effective approach to not only suppress volume variations but also facilitate Na<sup>+</sup>-ion transfer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 28 to 31">(28−31)</a> Nevertheless, using nanomaterials alone as an active material may cause high exposure to the liquid electrolyte, which can result in formation of a thick solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and agglomeration of particles during repeated cycles. To cope with the above issues of nanostructured materials and utilize the structural advantage of SnS, confining SnS QDs within a carbon matrix is a possible strategy. Furthermore, the strong connection between nanostructured active materials within a conductive carbon matrix can expeditiously accommodate volume changes and enhance the structural integrity, thereby improving the electrochemical performance compared with their solid counterparts. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 32 to 36">(32−36)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Considering the above points, we report ultrasmall SnS QDs anchored onto nitrogen-enriched carbon (SnS QDs@NC) nanospheres as a potential anode material for SIBs. These were synthesized through facile hydrothermal and carbonization reactions using polydopamine (PDA) as carbon and nitrogen sources. For comparison, pristine SnS QDs, NC, and SnS QDs anchored onto NC spheres with different sizes were also prepared. Systematic electrochemical measurements demonstrated that the prepared SnS QDs@NC-2 (for simplicity, SnS QDs@NC) electrode exhibited the best electrochemical performance in terms of cycling stability, reversible capacity, and rate capability.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Preparation of Nitrogen-Enriched Carbon (NC) Nanospheres</h3><div class="NLM_p last">First, PDA nanospheres were synthesized as follows. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> Briefly, 2 mL of aqueous ammonia was added to a mixture of deionized (DI) water and ethanol. Then, 0.5 g of dopamine hydrochloride was dissolved in 10 mL of DI water in a separate beaker. The dopamine solution was slowly added into the above mixed solvent with continuous stirring. Finally, the mixture was held at room temperature for 40 h for auto-oxidation. After this reaction, the obtained dark brown product was centrifuged, washed with DI water and ethyl alcohol several times to remove impurities, and dried at 80 °C overnight. NC nanospheres were prepared by annealing PDA spheres at 500 °C for 2 h under an argon atmosphere at a heating rate of 2 °C min<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Preparation of SnS QDs Anchored onto NC (SnS QDs@NC) Nanospheres</h3><div class="NLM_p last">SnS QDs were anchored onto NC nanospheres via a facile hydrothermal method. Briefly, 0.25 g of PDA spheres were dispersed in a DI water and ethylene glycol mixture (1:1 by volume ratio). Subsequently, a proper amount of SnCl<sub>2</sub> and thiourea was added to the above PDA solution. Then, the solution was heated to 160 °C for 4 h after transferring to a Teflon-lined autoclave. After reaction, it was cooled to room temperature. The obtained SnS QDs@PDA precipitate was washed several times with water and ethanol and dried at 80 °C. The SnS QDs@PDA nanospheres were annealed at 500 °C for 2 h at a heating rate of 2 °C min<sup>–1</sup> under an argon atmosphere to obtain SnS QDs@NC nanospheres. Pristine SnS QDs were also prepared by the same procedure, except for addition of PDA spheres. For comparison, SnS QDs@NC-1 and SnS QDs@NC-3 were also prepared by adding 1 and 3 mL of aqueous ammonia, respectively, during preparation of PDA spheres.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Structural Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">XRD patterns were obtained using a D8 Bruker X-ray diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation at a wavelength of 1.5405 Å in the 2θ range between 15 and 80°. Structural morphology and elemental mapping were investigated using a scanning electron microscope (NOVA Nano SEM-450) integrated with energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS). A high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HR-TEM) was used to identify the size of QDs, and the interlayer spacing was determined using a JEOL JEM 2100F system. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra were obtained using a VG Multilab ESCA system (Model: 220i) with Mg/Al Kα radiation. The carbon content was calculated based on thermogravimetric analysis (Auto-DSCQ20 system SDT Q600).</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Electrode Preparation and Cell Assembly</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Using water as solvent, the active material (SnS QDs, NC, or SnS QDs@NC), super-P, and CMC binder (carboxy methyl cellulose) were ground in a weight ratio of 70:15:15. The slurry was then coated onto Cu foil and dried at 80 °C overnight under vacuum. Approximately 2.0 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> of an active material was loaded onto the electrode. A CR2032-type coin cell was assembled using the prepared electrode and sodium foil (Alfa Aesar 99%), sandwiching a glass fiber separator (Whatman CAT No. 1823-047). NaClO<sub>4</sub> (1 M) in ethylene carbonate/propylene carbonate (50:50 by volume) with 10 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate (battery grade, PANAX ETEC Co., Ltd.) was injected into the cell. Karl Fischer titration was carried out using a Mettler-Toledo coulometer to confirm that the water content in the liquid electrolyte was less than 20 ppm. All cells were assembled in a glovebox under an argon atmosphere.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Electrochemical Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy and cyclic voltammetry (CV) were carried out using a CH instrument (CHI 600D Electrochemical Workshop) at 25 °C. Charge and discharge cycling tests were performed using battery test equipment (WBCS 3000, WonA Tech Co., Ltd.) at 25 °C. The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was performed to estimate the diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i>) of sodium ion using the equation <i>D</i> = [4<i>L</i><sup>2</sup>/(τ × π)][Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub>/Δ<i>E</i><sub>t</sub>]<sup>2</sup> where <i>L</i> is the thickness of the electrode (cm), τ is the relaxation time (s), Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> is the two consequent stabilized open-circuit potentials (V), and Δ<i>E</i><sub>t</sub> is the potential change after the <i>iR</i> drop during the pulse time (V). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> A sodium-ion full cell was fabricated using an SnS QDs@NC anode and a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode with an optimized mass ratio of 1.0:2.5.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">SnS QDs@NC nanospheres were prepared from the PDA template with SnCl<sub>2</sub> and thiourea precursors, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/medium/am9b18997_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0007.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of the stepwise preparation of SnS QDs@NC nanospheres.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b18997&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Briefly, PDA nanospheres were synthesized by addition of dopamine hydrochloride into an ethanol/water mixture at room temperature. The size of the nanospheres was controlled by adjusting the amount of ammonium hydroxide solution. The PDA nanospheres were reacted with tin chloride and thiourea precursors through a hydrothermal reaction to produce SnS QDs anchored onto PDA nanospheres (i.e., SnS QDs@PDA). Here, the active surface functional groups in PDA, such as hydroxyl (−OH) and amine (−NH<sub>2</sub>) groups, help to effectively anchor the Sn ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The formation of SnS QDs can be explained as follows. First, a Sn–ethylene glycol complex was formed by interactions between Sn<sup>2+</sup> and ethylene glycol. When thiourea was added into the Sn–ethylene glycol complex, the strong Sn–thiourea complex was developed by replacing ethylene glycol from the Sn–ethylene glycol complex. This strong bond hinders formation of free S<sup>2–</sup> ions in the solution. Upon hydrothermal treatment, the strongly coordinated Sn–thiourea was subjected to decomposition to produce SnS QDs. After a carbonization reaction, the PDA nanospheres were converted into carbon nanospheres enriched with nitrogen.</div><div class="NLM_p">As shown in the XRD patterns of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, all the peaks for the as-prepared SnS QDs were well matched with the orthorhombic structure of SnS (JCPDS: 01-073-1859), without any other peaks related to the Sn metal. The crystallite size of pristine SnS QDs based on the high-intensity peak was calculated using the Debye–Scherer equation (<i>D</i> = 0.9λ/β cos θ where <i>D</i> is the crystallite size, λ is the wavelength, β is the full-width at half maximum (FWHM) of the high intensity peak, and θ is the angle of diffraction). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The calculated crystallite size of SnS QDs was 18 nm. The XRD pattern of the pristine NC microsphere shows a broad peak around 24°, which reveals its amorphous nature. For the XRD pattern of SnS QDs@NC, almost all the peaks of pristine SnS were observed with reduced intensity due to the existence of amorphous carbon, which was confirmed based on the broadened peak around 24° (similar to pristine NC). This clearly demonstrated that SnS QDs were well anchored onto NC nanospheres. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b shows the distorted orthorhombic crystal structure of SnS. In the distorted structure, the Sn atom is strongly coordinated by six S atoms within the layer with three short Sn–S bonds and three long Sn–S bonds in the next layer. The lines joining the Sn atom with intralayer S atoms are relatively perpendicular to one another; this is also true for the interlayer S atoms. This clearly indicates that the layered structures are coordinated along the <i>c</i> axis by weak van der Waals forces. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> To investigate the interactions of SnS QDs with NC in SnS QDs@NC nanospheres, XPS was performed for pristine SnS QDs, NC, and SnS QDs@NC samples. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/suppl_file/am9b18997_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> shows the survey spectra of all the samples between binding energies of 0 and 800 eV. The Sn3d and S2p core-level spectra of SnS QDs and SnS QDs@NC are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c,d. Two peaks at 495.5 and 487.1 eV appeared in the Sn3d core-level spectrum of pristine SnS QDs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c) and can be assigned to Sn3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Sn3d<sub>5/2</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Additionally, two peaks at 162.1 and 160.9 eV appeared in the S2p spectrum of SnS QDs, attributed to S2p<sub>1/2</sub> and S2p<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a> These two peaks were slightly shifted toward higher binding energies for SnS QDs@NC and centered at 162.5 and 161.2 eV, respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e shows the C1s core-level spectra of NC and SnS QDs@NC. As shown in the figure, three major peaks in NC were observed at 283.8, 284.6, and 285.5 eV, which indicate the presence of sp<sup>2</sup>C carbon (C═C), sp<sup>3</sup>C carbon (C–C), and C–N/C–O, respectively. Also, C═O/C═N was spotted at a higher binding energy. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> Similar to the S2p characteristic peak, these assigned peaks for C1s were shifted toward higher binding energies in SnS QDs@NC. The three peaks at 398.3, 399.3, and 400.4 eV in the N1s spectrum of NC (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f) correspond to pyridinic-N (N1), pyrrolic-N (N2), and graphitic-N (N3). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Shifting of these peaks was also observed in SnS QDs@NC nanospheres. The shifts in SnS QDs@NC arise from the increased electron cloud density around SnS. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a> Simultaneously, the typical peak of the C1s spectrum in the SnS QDs@NC sample was upshifted to a higher binding energy than that of the NC nanosphere, which signify that the clouds of electrons around SnS are shared by the raised oxidation state of the carbon atoms. This result suggests that the tendencies of SnS and N-carbon evolved from the intimate alliance between higher electronegativity and distinct valence-band potentials of tin and carbon atoms, thereby creating a robust association and developing a heterojunction between the SnS QDs and NC nanospheres. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/medium/am9b18997_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0006.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD patterns of the SnS QDs, NC, and SnS QDs@NC nanospheres. (b) Crystal structure of SnS. XPS spectra of (c) Sn3d, (d) S2p, (e) C1s, and (f) N1s core levels in the SnS QDs, NC, and SnS QDs@ NC samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b18997&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">SEM images of all the prepared samples are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/suppl_file/am9b18997_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. All the samples clearly show uniform spherical particle shapes. As can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a,b, the size of the NC particle was ∼330 nm. Interestingly, the spherical NC particles were not destroyed under hydrothermal conditions with Sn and S precursors or during the carbonization reaction. It can be clearly seen from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c that the surface of an NC sphere was covered with tiny SnS particles. Meanwhile, FE-SEM images of NC-1 and NC-3, as well as corresponding samples with SnS QDs, are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/suppl_file/am9b18997_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. It is clear that the size of the NC spheres varied according to the amount of NH<sub>4</sub>OH present during polymerization of dopamine. The sizes of the spheres were measured to be 390 and 240 nm when using 1 and 3 mL of aqueous ammonia, respectively. The size of the SnS QDs@NC microstructures was further studied based on transmission electron microscope analysis. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d,e, smooth and well-structured spherical particles of NC were observed. The inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d shows a TEM image of pristine SnS QDs. The SAED pattern of NC (inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e) depicts cloudy patterns, which can be attributed to its amorphous nature. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f shows the TEM image of the SnS QDs@NC microstructures. It is apparent that the SnS QDs were homogeneously dispersed over the surface of the NC sphere. The SAED pattern of SnS QDs@NC (inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f) displays a cloudy pattern with mild polycrystalline nature. According to the TEM image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f, the size of the SnS QDs was measured to be 2–4 nm. HR-TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>g,h) show the well anchored SnS QDs with a lattice fringe of 0.28 nm (which corresponds to the (111) lattice plane of SnS) on the NC sphere. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>i and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/suppl_file/am9b18997_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> present the EDS mapping and corresponding EDS results, respectively. From these results, we could confirm the distribution of SnS over the surface of the NC sphere, as well as the presence of Sn, S, C, and N in the sphere. For comparison, the EDS spectrum and elemental mapping of the NC sphere are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/suppl_file/am9b18997_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. It confirms the presence of C, O, and N elements in the NC sphere. To estimate the weight ratio of NC and SnS QDs, thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was performed; these results are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/suppl_file/am9b18997_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. All the samples were heated to 700 °C at a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> under an air atmosphere. For pristine SnS QDs, no loss in weight was observed until 450 °C. Above 450 °C, weight gain started and continued until 650 °C; this result can be ascribed to partial oxidation of SnS QDs without loss of S atoms. It is due to the binding ability of more anions of tin atoms that have the grasping tendency of both +2 and +4 charges. A further increase in temperature caused weight loss back to the initial weight due to complete oxidation of SnS into SnO<sub>2</sub>; the molecular weight of SnS is equal to that of SnO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Unlike pristine SnS QDs, the SnS QDs@NC microstructures displayed significant weight loss due to oxidative decomposition of NC spheres. Due to the zero weight loss of SnS QDs, the carbon content in SnS QDs@NC was calculated to be 48%; in contrast, 54 and 51% carbon contents were observed for SnS QDs@NC-1 and SnS QDs@NC-3, respectively.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/medium/am9b18997_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0005.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. FE-SEM images of (a, b) NC nanospheres with different magnifications and (c) SnS QDs@NC nanospheres. TEM images of (d, e) NC nanospheres with different magnifications and (f) an SnS QDs@NC nanosphere. SnS QDs are shown in the inset of (d), an SAED pattern of the NC nanosphere is shown in the inset of (e), and an SnS QDs@NC nanosphere is shown in the inset of (f). (g, h) HR-TEM images of SnS QDs@NC nanospheres and (i) elemental mapping of SnS QDs@NC nanospheres.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b18997&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The sodium storage performance of the prepared electrodes was evaluated in the voltage range between 0.01 and 3.0 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the cyclic voltammogram (CV) of pristine SnS QDs. As shown in the figure, there was a strong reduction peak at 0.6 V during the first sodiation process. According to the literature, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> the Na–Sn alloying reaction takes place at a comparatively low potential region (less than 0.8 V). Therefore, the reduction peak at 0.6 V can be attributed to the alloying reaction of Na and Sn and SEI formation. During the second reduction scan, this broad peak was split into two separate peaks with a slight shift toward higher voltages at 1.07 and 0.68 V. The peak at 1.07 V can be ascribed to the SnS to Sn and Na<sub>2</sub>S conversion reaction, while the peak at 0.68 V is associated with the alloying reaction (multistep) of Sn where Na<sup>+</sup> forms Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Sn. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> The first two peaks at 0.27 and 0.76 V in the oxidation process can be assigned to the dealloying reaction of the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Sn alloy into metallic Sn. The third oxidation peak at 1.07 V arises from reverse conversion of SnS from Sn and Na<sub>2</sub>S, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> and the shoulder peak at 1.68 V comes from the reaction between Na and S in the −C–S–C– bond of the carbon scaffold. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> Such characteristics during the initial cycles mainly indicate the inevitable formation of an SEI and activation of the active material. In addition, the oxidation/reduction peaks observed at 0.29/0.11, 0.72/0.68, and 1.35/1.07 V for consecutive cycles are obvious, indicating good reversibility of the electrode materials. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b presents the cyclic voltammogram of the SnS QDs@NC electrode. It is apparent that the same kinds of peaks are observed compared to the pristine SnS electrode. However, there are some additional characteristics in the cyclic voltammogram of SnS QDs@NC. First, the peaks in the SnS QDs@NC electrode are well defined with a reduced overpotential compared to those of the pristine SnS electrode. Additionally, CVs of the SnS QDs@NC electrode are well overlapped with each other in subsequent cycles, indicating better stability of the SnS QDs@NC electrode. As presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/suppl_file/am9b18997_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a,b</a>, other SnS QDs@NC electrodes show similar behavior to the abovementioned electrode.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/medium/am9b18997_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Cyclic voltammograms of (a) SnS QDs and (b) SnS QDs@NC electrodes. (c) AC impedance spectra of SnS QDs and SnS QDs@NC electrodes. (d) Charge and discharge curves of the SnS QDs@NC electrode against sodium metal.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b18997&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further evaluate the electrochemical performance of SnS and SnS QDs@NC electrodes, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy was carried out, and the results are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c. As we can see from the figure, each AC impedance spectrum consists of a semicircle in the high-to-middle frequency and a subsequent inclined line at a lower frequency range; these results correspond to the charge-transfer reaction and the diffusion process of sodium ions, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> The measured charge transfer resistances (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) are 380 and 540 Ω for SnS and SnS QDs@NC, respectively; this difference may arise from the higher electronic conductivity of pristine SnS than SnS QDs@NC. However, the SnS QDs@NC electrode exhibited better sodium ion diffusion than SnS, as confirmed by the low-frequency slanted line parallel to the imaginary axis. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d shows the galvanostatic charge and discharge curves of the SnS QDs@NC electrode for the first three cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (two formation cycles) and 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (one cycle). The voltage plateaus observed during charge and discharge cycles are in good agreement with the redox peaks observed in the cyclic voltammograms of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b. The initial charge and discharge capacities are 489 and 300 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 61.3%. Similarly, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/suppl_file/am9b18997_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6c,d</a> shows the cycling curves of SnS QDs@NC-1 and SnS QDs@NC-3, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">Cyclic testing was performed for NC, SnS QDs, and SnS QDs@NC at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after two formation cycles (50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), and these results are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. In the first cycle, the initial Coulombic efficiencies of SnS QDs, NC, and SnS QDs@NC were 63.7, 49.1, and 61.3%, respectively. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, the SnS QDs and NC electrodes exhibited initial discharge capacities of 337 and 143 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, whereas the SnS QDs@NC electrode delivered an initial discharge capacity of 280 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. The SnS QDs electrode showed a slight increase in discharge capacity during the initial cycles due to activation of electrodes. After that, capacity fading began, and only 29 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was observed at the 200th cycle, which is due to pulverization of the alloy-type, Sn-based electrode. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/suppl_file/am9b18997_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a,b</a> shows the cycling stability of SnS QDs@NC-1 and SnS QDs@NC-3, respectively. After 200 cycles, discharge capacities of 235 and 204 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> were obtained for SnS QDs@NC-1 and SnS QDs@NC-3, respectively, revealing the improved cycling performance compared to the SnS QD electrode. The SnS QDs@NC electrode exhibited excellent cycling stability to 200 cycles. At the 200th cycle, a discharge capacity of 281 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was achieved for SnS QDs@NC, which corresponds to a capacity retention of 100% with a Coulombic efficiency &gt;99.0%. The rate capabilities of the NC, SnS QDs, and SnS QDs@NC electrodes are investigated by varying the current densities from 50 to 4000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b,c). When increasing the current density to 4000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the discharge capacity of the SnS electrode was significantly decreased to 134 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, whereas the SnS QDs@NC electrode delivered an improved discharge capacity of 172 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. The improved rate performance of the SnS QDs@NC electrode was attributed to a cooperative effect between SnS QDs and NC nanospheres. When the current density was returned to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after cycling at 4000 mAg<sup>–1</sup>, the SnS QDs@NC electrode recovered its initial discharge capacity, indicating no significant structural deterioration in the active material during high-rate cycling. We also evaluated the rate capability of SnS QDs@NC-1 and SnS QDs@NC-3 electrodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/suppl_file/am9b18997_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7c,d</a>). When the current density was returned to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> from the higher current, the discharge capacity recovered. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d shows the AC impedance spectra of the SnS QD electrode before and after cycling, which signifies that the charge transfer resistance was increased by the large volume expansion of pristine SnS QDs. On the other hand, the charge transfer resistance was reduced after 200 cycles in the SnS QDs@NC electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e), indicating that the electrochemical activity of the SnS QDs@NC electrode slightly increased during the repeated cycles. This result also reveals that attachment of the electrode to the current collector was well preserved during continuous charge and discharge cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> The cycling stability of the SnS QDs@NC electrode was evaluated at a high current density of 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. As depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f, the SnS QDs@NC electrode delivered a high discharge capacity of 172 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> even after 500 cycles at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which was 75% of its initial discharge capacity. When comparing with other metal sulfide-based anode materials for sodium-ion batteries, the SnS QDs@NC electrode in this work exhibited comparable cycling stability and rate capability, as presented in Table S1.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/medium/am9b18997_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0001.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Cycling performance of SnS QDs, NC, and SnS QDs@NC electrodes at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after two formation cycles. (b) Rate capability of SnS QDs, NC, and SnS QDs@NC electrodes at various current densities. (c) Charge and discharge curves of the SnS QDs@NC electrode at different current densities. AC impedance spectra of (d) SnS QDs and (e) SnS QDs@NC electrodes after formation and 200 cycles. (f) Cycling stability of the SnS QDs@NC electrode at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b18997&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To understand the enhanced electrochemical performance of the SnS QDs@NC electrode, its electrochemical kinetic behavior was investigated. First, GITT was performed to calculate the diffusion coefficients of sodium ion at different stages of sodiation and desodiation, and the results are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a,b. The initial overpotential during the first sodiation can be ascribed to the decomposition of electrolyte and subsequent formation of the SEI film. The diffusion coefficients of sodium ions in the SnS QDs@NC electrode are relatively high and almost the same during sodiation and desodiation processes. This result is primarily attributed to the presence of carbon nanospheres that provide an efficient conduction pathway through penetration of electrolyte to improve the sodium ion accessibility. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/medium/am9b18997_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0002.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Kinetic study of SnS QDs@NC electrodes: (a) GITT curves and corresponding sodium-ion diffusion coefficient during the first sodiation and desodiation process, (b) <i>E</i> vs <i>t</i> profile of single GITT during the sodiation process, (c) CV curves at various scan rates, (d) linear fit of the logarithmic scale of current (<i>i</i>) vs scan rate (<i>v</i>), (e) capacitive contribution at a scan rate of 0.4 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, and (f) relative capacitive and diffusion contribution at various scan rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b18997&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Second, cyclic voltammetry was performed at different scan rates in the range of 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c). All the CV curves are well preserved with slight shifting when increasing the scan rate. The logarithmic scale of current versus scan rate was plotted to estimate the <i>b</i> value in the equation (<i>i</i> = <i>a</i> × <i>v<sup>b</sup></i>), as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d. The capacitive- and diffusion-governed phenomenon could be estimated from the value of <i>b</i> (<i>b</i> = 1: capacitive dominant, <i>b</i> = 0.5: diffusion dominant). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> The calculated <i>b</i> values at anodic and cathodic regions are 0.87 and 0.80, respectively, indicating the dominance of surface capacitive behavior. To be more accurate, the capacitive contributions can be derived from another well-known equation (<i>i</i> = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>ν + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>ν<sup>1/2</sup>) where ν is the scan rate and <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>ν and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>ν<sup>1/2</sup> stand for the dominance of capacitive and diffusion governed reactions, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e,f). As a result, the obtained capacitive contributions are 29.4, 42.1, 55.8, 60.6, 68.7, and 71.2% at scan rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. These results demonstrate that the sodium storage behavior of the SnS QDs@NC electrode was mainly governed by surface capacitive-controlled reactions.</div><div class="NLM_p">It is important to perform the post-mortem analysis of the electrodes to understand the electrochemical performance and reaction kinetics. Ex situ FE-SEM measurements were performed for the SnS QDs and SnS QDs@NC electrodes after 200 cycles, and the resulting SEM images are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a–d. As can be seen from the top view images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a), there are many cracks on the surface of the SnS QD electrode after 200 cycles. Especially, the high-magnification image shows a large crack with a size &gt;1 μm on the surface of the SnS QD electrode. Similarly, a cross-sectional FE-SEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b) exhibits the cracked and damaged surface, indicating that the SnS QD electrode was subjected to structural deterioration caused by significant strain. In contrast, the top view and cross-sectional images of the SnS QDs@NC electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c,d) show an almost smooth surface with only slight cracking and no significant damage to the spherical structure. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>e presents the cross-sectional EDS mappings of the SnS QDs@NC electrode after 200 cycles. As can be clearly seen from the images, the elements Sn, S, C, and N are uniformly distributed throughout the electrode, even after cycling.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/medium/am9b18997_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Top view FE-SEM image and (b) cross-sectional image of the SnS QDs electrode after 200 cycles and (c) top view FE-SEM image and (d) cross-sectional image of the SnS QDs@NC electrode after 200 cycles. (e) Electron image with elemental mapping of different elements in the SnS QDs@NC electrode after 200 cycles. (f, g) TEM images with different magnifications, (h) HR-TEM image (inset: SAED pattern) of the SnS QDs@NC electrode, and (i) corresponding elemental mapping of different elements in the SnS QDs@NC electrode after 200 cycles (scale bar: 100 nm).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b18997&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">TEM images and corresponding EDS mapping of the SnS QDs@NC electrode after 200 cycles are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>f,h,i, respectively. As shown in TEM images, the spherical nature of particles was well preserved even after 200 cycles, except aggregation of SnS QDs on the surface of NC nanospheres. The presence of SnS in HR-TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>h) after 200 cycles demonstrates their good reversible nature. Elemental mapping of SnS QDs@NC nanospheres shows that all elements are uniformly distributed after cycling. These results reveal the favorable outcome of homogeneously anchoring ultrasmall SnS QDs on the spherical N-doped carbon, which accommodates the volume inflation of SnS QDs during the repeated charge and discharge cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further understand the electrochemical processes of the SnS QDs@NC electrode, ex situ XRD and XPS analyses were performed at the different potentials during the initial charge and discharge cycle, and the results are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a,b, respectively. During the initial sodiation process, the peaks of SnS started to weaken and disappeared at 0.5 V. At the same time, the new peaks corresponding to Sn metal could be observed, which indicates the occurrence of conversion reaction between SnS and Na metal, resulting in the formation of Sn metal and amorphous Na<sub>2</sub>S. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> In the full sodiation state at 0.01 V, the Sn peak appeared. There is a chance to form the Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> phase through the alloying reaction; however, we could not detect the crystalline peak of Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> in the XRD pattern due to the low crystallinity and small particle size. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 51 and 52">(51,52)</a> During the desodiation process, the crystalline peaks of Sn metal were weakened and completely disappeared at a full desodiation state at 2.5 V. Similarly, XPS spectra of Sn3d and C1s core levels are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b. During sodiation, the Sn3d<sub>5/2</sub> peak was gradually shifted toward higher binding energy, whereas the Sn3d<sub>5/2</sub> peak was gradually weakened and disappeared at a full sodiation state (0.01 V). On the other hand, the C1s core-level spectrum showed similar shifting toward higher binding energy with increasing intensity of the C═O/C═N peak at high binding energy. It might be due to fact that the intercalation of sodium ions is relatively predominant with C═O/C═N bonds in carbon nanospheres. During desodiation, there was little difference in Sn3d, whereas a slight decrement in the intensity of C═O/C═N peak was noticed. This phenomenon was well consistent with the ex situ TEM and XRD analyses.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/medium/am9b18997_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0003.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a) Ex situ XRD patterns and (b) ex situ XPS spectra (Sn3d and C1s core levels) of the SnS QDs@NC electrode at different sodiation and desodiation voltages during the first charge and discharge process. (c) Charge and discharge curves of the sodium-ion full cell consisting of the SnS QDs@NC anode and NVP cathode at various cycles, and (d) cycling stability of the corresponding sodium-ion full cell at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Inset of (d) is a schematic presentation of a sodium-ion cell.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/asset/images/large/am9b18997_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b18997&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Inspired by the good electrochemical performance of the SnS QDs@NC electrode, we assembled a sodium-ion cell consisting of an SnS QDs@NC anode and a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode to scrutinize the feasibility of practical application. The mass ratio of the cathode to the anode was optimized to 2.5:1.0, and the specific capacity was calculated based on the anode material. A cycling test was performed at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after two pre-conditioning cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>c shows the charge and discharge curves of the sodium-ion full cell. An initial discharge capacity of 202 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was achieved with a Coulombic efficiency of 93.3%. The consecutive cycles were almost overlapped with a Coulombic efficiency of &gt;99.0%. As depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>d, a discharge capacity of 182 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was achieved at the 50th cycle, which corresponded to 90.1% of its initial discharge capacity. Based on these results, the SnS QDs@NC electrode can be a potential candidate for the anode material for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this study, we successfully synthesized ultrasmall SnS QDs anchored on nitrogen-enriched carbon nanospheres via facile hydrothermal and carbonization processes. The anchoring of SnS QDs onto the NC nanospheres was confirmed based on structural characterization. NC nanospheres with different sizes were prepared to determine the optimum SnS QDs@NC material with good electrochemical performance. Compared to the pristine SnS QDs and NC electrodes, the SnS QDs@NC electrode exhibited superior cycling stability (initial discharge capacity: 280 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, 100% retention) and improved rate capability (172 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 4000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). Ex situ XRD, XPS, and morphological analyses were performed to investigate the improved sodium storage performance. Kinetic analysis revealed that this ameliorated storage performance could be achieved by the enhanced diffusion of sodium ion during cycling and improved pseudocapacitive contribution. A sodium-ion full cell assembled with an SnS QDs@NC anode and a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode exhibited good cycling performance in terms of discharge capacity and cycling stability. This exceptional performance is associated with the synergistic effects of NC and SnS QDs where NC serves as a conducting scaffold to support SnS QDs and suppress structural degradation. Our results demonstrate the significance of homogeneously anchoring ultrasmall SnS QDs onto nitrogen-enriched carbon spheres, which buffers the volume inflation in conversion-alloying type anode materials. This technique allows us to resolve the pulverization issues of these active electrode materials.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b18997?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b18997</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XPS survey spectra of SnS QDs, NC and SnS QDs@NC; FE-SEM images of SnS QDs@NC-1 and SnS QDs@NC-3; EDS spectrum of SnS QDs@NC; elemental mapping and EDS spectrum of NC; TGA curves of SnS QDs, NC and SnS QDs@NC; cyclic voltammograms and charge–discharge curves of the SnS QDs@NC-1 and SnS QDs@NC-3 electrodes; cycling stability and rate capability of the SnS QDs@NC-1 and SnS QDs@NC-3 electrodes; and comparison of cycling performance of metal sulfide-based anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/suppl_file/am9b18997_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b18997/suppl_file/am9b18997_si_001.pdf">am9b18997_si_001.pdf (845.36 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b18997" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A scalable and flexible hybrid energy storage system design and implementation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Younghyun; Koh, Jason; Xie, Qing; Wang, Yanzhi; Chang, Naehyuck; Pedram, Massoud</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">255</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">410-422</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy storage systems (ESS) are becoming one of the most important components that noticeably change overall system performance in various applications, ranging from the power grid infrastructure to elec. vehicles and portable electronics.  However, a homogeneous ESS is subject to limited characteristics in terms of cost, efficiency, and lifetime, by the energy storage technol. that comprises the ESS.  On the other hand, hybrid ESS (HESS) are a viable soln. for a practical ESS with currently available technologies as they have potential to overcome such limitations by exploiting only advantages of heterogeneous energy storage technologies while hiding their drawbacks.  However, the HESS concept basically mandates sophisticated design and control to actually make the benefits happen.  The HESS architecture should be able to provide controllability of many parts, which are often fixed in homogeneous ESS, and novel management policies should be able to utilize the control features.  This paper introduces a complete design practice of a HESS prototype to demonstrate scalability, flexibility, and energy efficiency.  It is composed of three heterogeneous energy storage elements: lead-acid batteries, lithium-ion batteries, and supercapacitors.  We demonstrate a novel system control methodol. and enhanced energy efficiency through this design practice.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr0JsM1Kx-no7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhjpYzCzrPreg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXisFekuro%253D&amp;md5=6118a2715d543225c6c57725a48080e3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1126/science.1212741"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dunn, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kamath, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrical Energy Storage for the Grid: A Battery of Choices</span>. <i>Science</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2011</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>334</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">928</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">935</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZwDymuubZm7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhjpYzCzrPreg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhsVGktL%252FJ&amp;md5=5035bfda7631ad9d075e8836e61146ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2009.11.048"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Scrosati, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Garche, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lithium Batteries: Status, Prospects and Future</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium batteries are characterized by high specific energy, high efficiency and long life.  These unique properties have made lithium batteries the power sources of choice for the consumer electronics market with a prodn. of the order of billions of units per yr.  These batteries are also expected to find a prominent role as ideal electrochem. storage systems in renewable energy plants, as well as power systems for sustainable vehicles, such as hybrid and elec. vehicles.  However, scaling up the lithium battery technol. for these applications is still problematic since issues such as safety, costs, wide operational temp. and materials availability, are still to be resolved.  This review focuses first on the present status of lithium battery technol., then on its near future development and finally it examines important new directions aimed at achieving quantum jumps in energy and power content.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpF3KOlj2NuArVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liPPzVdrfzxKw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXivF2htQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=7273fa7f9f8dd9462c29aac1df0c7420</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1038/451652a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Armand, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Building Better Batteries</span>. <i>Nature</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2008</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>451</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">652</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">657</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">P2-type Nax[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2 made from earth-abundant elements for rechargeable Na batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Kajiyama, Masataka; Iwatate, Junichi; Nishikawa, Heisuke; Hitomi, Shuji; Okuyama, Ryoichi; Usui, Ryo; Yamada, Yasuhiro; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
